        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.2c06295"><meta property="og:title" content="Improved Electrochemical Performance of NTs-WS2@C Nanocomposites for Lithium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/acsami.2c06295.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Even though WS2 nanotubes (NTs-WS2) have great potential as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) thanks to their unusual layered structure, their conductivity and cycling stability are far from satisfactory. To tackle these issues, carbon-coated WS2 (NTs-WS2@C) nanocomposites were prepared through a facile synthesis method that involved precipitating a carbon precursor (20% sucrose) on WS2 nanotubes, followed by annealing treatment under an argon environment. Thanks to the presence of highly conductive and mechanically robust carbon on the outer surface, NTs-WS2@C nanocomposites show improved electrochemical performance compared with bare NTs-WS2. After 60 cycles at 80 mA g–1 current density, the cells display high capacities of 305 mAh g–1 in LIBs and 152 mAh g–1 in SIBs, respectively. As the current density increases to 600 mA g–1, it provides specific capacities of 209 and 115 mAh g–1, correspondingly. The enhanced electrochemical performance in LIBs and SIBs is primarily attributed to the synergistic effects of the tubular architecture of WS2, carbon network and stable nanocomposite structure, which can effectively constrain volume variation during the metal ions intercalation/deintercalation processes."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/acsami.2c06295.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318819c1a9254b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318819c1a9254b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '7030008e-89a4-43f4-84e3-b77e271c1f98'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318819c1a9254b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="9318819c1a9254b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improved Electrochemical Performance of NTs-WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanocomposites for Lithium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c06295", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improved Electrochemical Performance of NTs-WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanocomposites for Lithium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c06295", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improved Electrochemical Performance of NTs-WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanocomposites for Lithium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c06295", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improved Electrochemical Performance of NTs-WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanocomposites for Lithium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c06295", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improved Electrochemical Performance of NTs-WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanocomposites for Lithium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c06295", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improved Electrochemical Performance of NTs-WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanocomposites for Lithium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c06295", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improved Electrochemical Performance of NTs-WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanocomposites for Lithium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c06295", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improved Electrochemical Performance of NTs-WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanocomposites for Lithium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c06295", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Improved Electrochemical Performance of NTs-WS&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanocomposites for Lithium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c06295", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/medium/am2c06295_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06295&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Even though WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes (NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>) have great potential as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) thanks to their unusual layered structure, their conductivity and cycling stability are far from satisfactory. To tackle these issues, carbon-coated WS<sub>2</sub> (NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C) nanocomposites were prepared through a facile synthesis method that involved precipitating a carbon precursor (20% sucrose) on WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes, followed by annealing treatment under an argon environment. Thanks to the presence of highly conductive and mechanically robust carbon on the outer surface, NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C nanocomposites show improved electrochemical performance compared with bare NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>. After 60 cycles at 80 mA g<sup>–1</sup> current density, the cells display high capacities of 305 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in LIBs and 152 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in SIBs, respectively. As the current density increases to 600 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, it provides specific capacities of 209 and 115 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, correspondingly. The enhanced electrochemical performance in LIBs and SIBs is primarily attributed to the synergistic effects of the tubular architecture of WS<sub>2</sub>, carbon network and stable nanocomposite structure, which can effectively constrain volume variation during the metal ions intercalation/deintercalation processes.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D46386%26pageCount%3D15%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DShuangying%2BWei%252C%2BMarco%2BSerra%252C%2BStefanos%2BMourdikoudis%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D14%26issueNum%3D41%26contentID%3Dacsami.2c06295%26title%3DImproved%2BElectrochemical%2BPerformance%2Bof%2BNTs-WS2%2540C%2BNanocomposites%2Bfor%2BLithium-Ion%2Band%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D15%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D46400%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2022" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2022 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291646&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon nanotubes"> Carbon nanotubes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291557&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Coating materials"> Coating materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties"> Electrical properties </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Tungsten+Disulfides&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_tungsten%20disulfides">tungsten disulfides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nanotubes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nanotubes">nanotubes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon+Coating&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbon%20coating">carbon coating</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Lithium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_lithium%20ion%20batteries">lithium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">As fossil fuels become depleted, the search for renewable energy sources, such as solar, hydropower, wind, biomass, and tidal resources, has intensified in the past decade. This is due to the appealing characteristics of these energy forms in terms of huge development potential, green, pollution-free use, recyclability, and low exploitation costs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> In this context, the development of more advanced large-scale electrical energy storage technologies is critical. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3,4)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Some of the most suitable energy storage systems include lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7,8)</a> Among them, LIBs are already employed in several daily activities and industrial fields, ranging from portable electronic gadgets to grid-scale storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9−11)</a> As a result of their high energy density and reliability, it is expected that LIBs will be used in large-scale applications, including pure electric vehicles (EVs), hybrid electric vehicles (HEVs), and hybrid plug-in electric vehicles (HPEVs). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> On the other hand, SIBs have garnered much research interest as an excellent alternative to LIBs thanks to their abundant sodium resources, suitable redox potential, low cost, and similar storage mechanism to LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> However, Na<sup>+</sup> ions have a larger radius (1.06 Å vs 0.76 Å for Li<sup>+</sup>), heavier molar mass (23.0 g mol<sup>–1</sup> for Na vs 6.9 g mol<sup>–1</sup> for Li), and higher ionization potential, meaning that SIBs face challenges, for example,  delayed ion transport and reaction kinetics that diminish their energy outputs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 15 and 16">(15,16)</a> As a result, the selection of suitable electrode materials remains tricky. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The anode material, the principal component of a lithium-ion cell, plays a vital role in limiting the energy density and accounts for 10–15% of the overall battery cost. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> The first commercialized anode material, graphite, has been extensively studied, but numerous inherent disadvantages, such as limited rate capability and theoretical capacity (372 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), make its long-term practical deployment rather complicated. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Other carbon-based materials, particularly multiwalled carbon nanotubes and graphene, have shown promising performance, with reversible capacities between 250 and 750 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> Nonetheless, these anode materials still have relatively low energy densities, which are insufficient to address the increasing demand for energy storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> Therefore, the development of higher capacity anode materials is of crucial importance for the deployment of LIBs and SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Due to their unique layered structure and excellent energy storage performance, 2D transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs), which have the general formula M<i>X</i><sub>2</sub> (M = Co, Fe, Ni, Mo, W, Ti, or V; <i>X</i> = S or Se), are being widely investigated as anode materials for LIBs and SIBs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26,27)</a> particularly layer-structured tungsten disulfide (WS<sub>2</sub>). Due to its higher theoretical capacity (∼433 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> vs 372 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for graphite) and larger interlayer spacing (0.62 nm vs 0.34 nm for graphite), WS<sub>2</sub> is capable of hosting Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> ions, which offers beneficial conditions for rapid intercalation and deintercalation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Moreover, the presence of weak van der Waals interactions between WS<sub>2</sub> layers, enables it to form graphene-like structures (thin nanosheets), fullerene-like (semispherical or polyhedral) and nanotube (tubular) ones. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Actually, several works have reported the preparation and electrode application of 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> (hexagonal) platelets; <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> results demonstrated that 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> has poor electrical conductivity and serious volume variation during cycling, narrowing its practical applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Various approaches have been proposed to enhance the electrochemical performance of 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> flakes. These include partial elemental doping/substitution, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> carbon coating, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> morphological tailoring, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> and nanoarchitecture. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> One of the most used strategies is to prepare carbon-WS<sub>2</sub> hybrids; this involves different carbonaceous sources, such as ordered mesoporous carbon, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> glucose, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> citric acid, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> and carbon spheres, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> which can improve the electrical conductivity of the materials. Sucrose, with the chemical formula C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>22</sub>O<sub>11</sub>, is deemed to be one of the best carbon sources for the homogeneous surface coating. In fact, 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> flakes have been widely studied for LIBs or SIBs, whereas the Li-storage and Na-storage performances of WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes (NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>) have rarely been explored in detail. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> These nanotubes have closed cage structure, which bestows them chemical stability and limits volume expansion. Furthermore, we may take advantage of the nanostructure properties (a large specific surface area, favorable electrical conductivity, and desired highly active sites) of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>, which are not presented in the platelets. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The carbon film can be formed around the surface of the nanotubes via a carbonization process. Indeed, carbon coating is a popular technique; however, in current work the carbon coating was done on the nanotubes and not on the platelets as previously. In this regard, utilizing NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> for preparation of carbon-WS<sub>2</sub> hybrids may combine the distinct properties of the nanotube configuration with the carbonaceous film. Herein, the conformal coating of the nanotubes by a thin carbonaceous film provided extra cycling stability and improved the electronic conductivity of the nanocomposites.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In line with this trend, our simple, fast, and inexpensive production method involves precipitation of sucrose precursor on WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes and their subsequent pyrolysis, forming carbon-coated NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> (NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C) nanocomposites. The crystal structure, morphology, and composition of the produced NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C nanocomposites were thoroughly studied using X-ray diffraction (XRD), X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), scanning electron microscopy (SEM), transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and high-angle annular dark field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM-EDX) techniques. The metal ions intercalation/deintercalation processes of the electrode were investigated by means of cyclic voltammetry (CV), electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and galvanostatic charge/discharge measurements. Moreover, to the best of our knowledge, this is the first time  sucrose was employed as an organic carbon source for the coating of one-dimensional (1D) WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes. Such a facile synthesis method resulted in the formation of pure NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C products in high yield. Therefore, this type of carbon coating provides an attractive alternative strategy for designing and modifying WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes. The NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C nanocomposites exhibited remarkable electrochemical properties that mark them attractive anode materials for LIBs and SIBs. Commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> powder was used as a reference for comparing the electrochemical performance and then for emphasizing the novelty of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Result and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The XRD spectra of the two principal samples (NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C) are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a. The peaks of pristine NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> correspond well with the (International Centre for Diffraction Data-Powder Diffraction File) ICDD-PDF card of hexagonal WS<sub>2</sub> (No. 08-0237). The diffractogram of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C is identical to that of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>, pointing that the carbon coating processes did not affect the crystallinity of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and no new crystalline phases were generated during the calcination step. Furthermore, any carbon diffraction peaks resulting from sucrose were not observed due to the minimal amount of carbon present on the particle. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/medium/am2c06295_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns, (b) Raman spectra, and (c) TGA curves of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C. (d and e) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm curves of (d) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and (e) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C. (f) Pore size distributions of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06295&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b presents the Raman spectra of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C. The two characteristic bands located at 351 and 419 cm<sup>–1</sup> are assigned to the typical in-plane E<sub class="stack">2g</sub><sup class="stack">1</sup> and out-of-plane A<sub>1g</sub> vibration modes of WS<sub>2</sub>, respectively, indicating on the preservation of high crystallinity of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> after carbon coating and thermal treatment. For the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C sample, the Raman signals at 1354 and 1600 cm<sup>–1</sup> correspond to the D-band and G-band of carbon, indicating on the signatures of sucrose-derived carbon after thermal treatment. The D-band is characterized by disorder originating from the edge or defected sites of the carbon structure, whereas the G-band can be associated with the sp<sup>2</sup> hybridization mode of the graphitic carbon structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The intensity ratio of the D-band peak to the G-band peak (I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub>) is related to the degree of graphitization. The I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C sample herein is estimated to be 0.85, implying the high disorder feature of carbon in NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> after pyrolysis at 550 °C for 8 h. In addition, the increase in defects and disordered regions caused by the introduction of a carbon coating in NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> can be conducive to electronic and ionic diffusion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> It is also worth noting that the mostly intensive Raman scattering peaks for NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C are observed below 1000 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which is in agreement with the crystalline nature of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>.</div><div class="NLM_p">TGA helps to identify the carbon content in NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C material considering that all carbon was oxidized to CO<sub>2</sub>, while WS<sub>2</sub> was oxidized to WO<sub>3</sub> and SO<sub>2</sub> during the heating in the presence of oxygen. NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C underwent a TGA measurement under the same conditions (30–850 °C) to obtain weight variation. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c, both materials lost about 1% of their weight up to 300 °C, which might be due to residual moisture and traces of sulfur. The major decomposition started for both materials at ≈400 °C and was finished at ≈570 °C without any additional process taking place when heated up to 850 °C. The total weight losses for NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C are 8% and 13%, respectively. The weight loss for pristine NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> implies that WS<sub>2</sub> was oxidized into WO<sub>3</sub> (theoretical weight loss 6.5%). The weight loss of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C is ≈13%, which reflects the WS<sub>2</sub> oxidation to WO<sub>3</sub> as well as the amorphous carbon oxidation to CO<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Based on the assumption that the residual phases after the completion of the TGA test are pure WO<sub>3</sub>; the carbon content in NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C is estimated to be ≈5%.</div><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d and e, N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption analyses of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C exhibit similar type IV isotherms followed by a H3 hysteresis loop, revealing their mesoporous structures; such a configuration can be attributed to the interlayer spaces and hollow core within WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes and the spaces between the intercrossed WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> The carbon coating of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> has a significant effect on the surface area and total pore volume, as shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>. The specific surface areas of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C are 19.19 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> (with pore size distributions centered at 24 and 27.07 nm) and 50.09 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> (characterized by the pore size distributions centered at 3.65 and 24 nm), respectively. It has to be highlighted that the measured surface area for NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C is higher (≈2.5 times), indicating better separation of the nanotubes after the hybridization-mixing process and, therefore, better deagglomeration and lower van der Waals attraction between carbon-coated nanotubes. In turn, the increased specific surface area may offer more reaction sites for ions migration and guarantee sufficient contact area between electrode and electrolyte. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> The pore size distribution of each sample was examined with the assistance of the (Barrett–Joyner–Halenda) BJH method (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f). When compared to NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> (0.596 cm<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), the introduction of a carbon coating results in a big  decrease (4 times) in total pore volume (0.138 cm<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>). Such porous structures may not only markedly minimize the diffusion path for Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> ions but also effectively reduce the volume change of electrode materials during the charge/discharge process, thereby improving the electrochemical kinetics. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. BET Surface Area, Total Pore Volume, and Mean Pore Size of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">Materials</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">BET surface area (m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">BJH pore volume (cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">Mean pore size (nm)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">NTs-WS<sub>2</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">19.19</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.596</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">61.31</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">50.09</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.138</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">57.79</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">SEM and TEM were used to examine the morphological features of the different samples (commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub>, NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>, and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>. The commercial WS<sub>2</sub> sample is formed by aggregates of interconnected micron-sized primary 2H crystals ranging from 1 to 5 μm, presenting a platelet-shaped configuration, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a. The pristine NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> sample has tubular shape with diameter distributions ranging typically from 50 to 400 nm and lengths ranging between 2 and 10 μm (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c reveals that, in NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C, the tubular structure of WS<sub>2</sub> was preserved after carbon coating and the diameters of the nanotubes were slightly decreased (50 to 200 nm). Meanwhile, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d shows the SEM-EDS elemental mappings of W, S, and C, which demonstrate the uniform distribution of all elements throughout the specimen.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/medium/am2c06295_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM images with two different magnifications of (a) commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub>, (b) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>, and (c) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C. (d) SEM-EDS element mapping images of W, S, and C of NT-WS<sub>2</sub>@C. (e–g, i, k) TEM and HRTEM images of (e–g) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and (i, k) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C. (j) HAADF-STEM-EDS element mapping images of W, S, and C of NT-WS<sub>2</sub>@C. (h and l) SAED patterns of (h) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and (l) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06295&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The TEM images illustrate a hollow tubular structure assembled by multiwall WS<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e–f). As shown previously, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref50" aria-label="reference 47 and 50">(47,50)</a> the interlayer spacing for nanotubes (inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e) usually exceeds the corresponding distance in bulk 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> (0.62 nm), which can be put down to the high levels of stress within the bent layered structure. High-resolution TEM demonstrates that the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> sample is highly crystalline and multilayered (10–15 layers), with an interlayer spacing of 0.63 nm, which corresponds to the (002) interplanar distance (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g).</div><div class="NLM_p">The presence of a carbon coating covering the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> uniformly is observed in inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>i. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>j displays the HAADF-STEM-EDS elemental mappings, which indicate that the W and S elements are evenly distributed within the WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes and the C on the nanotube surface. The HRTEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>k) indicates a layer distance of 0.64 nm, which refers to the (002) interplanar distance. The mostly multichiral nature of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C is implied by the corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>h and l), which show scattered diffraction spots rather than continuous diffraction rings. In addition, the amount of carbon (≈5 wt %) is too small and is just not visible compared to the strong diffraction from crystalline NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> shows the morphological features of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C individual nanotube at different sides of its volume (left, middle, and right). Due to the low carbon contents (≈5%) in NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C, there is no apparent difference in these TEM images, which reflects the fact that the overall crystallinity of the multiwall WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes is rather high.</div><div class="NLM_p">These findings (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>) suggest that the synthesis method and conditions used in this study can yield high-purity and high-quality NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C composite material. Moreover, the formation of a carbon coating in mild conditions (at 550 °C) has no deleterious effect on the integrity of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>, which could be beneficial for high-capacity conversion reactions during the lithiation/delithiation and sodiation/desodiation processes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">XPS analysis was carried out to shed light on the oxidation states of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C, as reported in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>. The XPS survey spectrum for NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C is presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, exhibiting W, S, and C elements, which correspond to the sample composition. The high-resolution XPS spectra of W 4f and S 2p confirm the existence of WS<sub>2</sub> compound, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b and c. One pair of peaks at 32.6 and 34.7 eV is observed in the core-level spectrum of W 4f, which can be assigned to the W 4f<sub>7/2</sub> and W 4f<sub>5/2</sub> characteristic peaks for the W–S bond. The W 4f<sub>7/2</sub> and W 4f<sub>5/2</sub> characteristic peaks for the W–O bond are represented by the other pair of peaks at 36.0 and 38.0 eV, which can be attributed to the inevitable partial surface oxidation of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C sample prior to XPS measurement. Besides, a peak at 38.4 eV is assigned to W 5p<sub>3/2</sub>. The NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C sample exhibits the doublet profile of S 2p, which is characteristic of the S<sup>2–</sup> species, at 162.3 and 163.5 eV, and is attributed to the 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively. The C 1s peaks of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C sample, as can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d, are attributed to the C═C, C–C, C–O, C═O, and O–C═O bonding states, which are centered at 284.3, 285.3, 286.2, 286.8, and 288.2 eV, respectively; this could be ascribed to residual oxygen-containing functional impurities. The XPS results are clearly consistent with the theoretical expectations in this regard. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/medium/am2c06295_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. High-resolution XPS spectra of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C: (a) survey spectrum, (b) W 4f, (c) S 2p, and (d) C 1s.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06295&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">CV and EIS measurements were conducted in two-electrode lithium and sodium half-cells over the voltage range of 0.01 to 3.0 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> to evaluate the metal storage  process of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes. Panels a and c in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a> depict three voltammetry cycles as well as the Nyquist plots documented at the open circuit voltage (OCV) and after three CV cycles in LIBs. Three cathodic peaks of pristine NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> appear at around 0.91, 0.71, and 0.61 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li in the first cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a), and similar peaks of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C appear at about 0.89, 0.68, and 0.59 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). These responses can be assigned to the insertion of Li<sup>+</sup> ions into WS<sub>2</sub>, the conversion reaction of WS<sub>2</sub> and Li<sup>+</sup> ions into W in a Li<sub>2</sub>S matrix, irreversible electrolyte decomposition, and the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The intense oxidation potential of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes respectively at 2.42 and 2.41 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li reflects the partial oxidation of W to WS<sub>2</sub>, corresponding to the lithium extraction processes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Such reduction potential between ∼0.9 and ∼0.6 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li shifts to higher values in the following cycles, i.e., to 2.06 and 1.83 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li as well as 2.08 and 1.84 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li, respectively, implying enhanced reversibility for the electrochemical lithiation and delithiation processes. Both electrodes show two highly reversible cathodic peaks at ∼2.10 and ∼1.80 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li, and one anodic peak at ∼2.40 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li, corresponding to the reversible lithium insertion and desertion processes, respectively. Despite the similar CV response, NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C display a rather negligible decrease in polarization within three CV cycles, and all peaks seem to be kept at almost the same positions as the initial ones, implying their high reversibility. Therefore, one may reasonably conclude that the presence of a carbon coating favors the enhancement of the electrochemical kinetics of the electrode.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/medium/am2c06295_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a and c) CV and impedance spectra (Nyquist plots, insets) of two-electrode lithium cells using (a) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and (c) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C as the working electrodes. (b and d) CV and impedance spectra (Nyquist plots, insets) of two-electrode sodium cells using (b) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and (d) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C as the working electrodes. CV conducted at 0.01–3.0 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li with a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. EIS measured at OCV and after the 1<sup>st</sup> and 3<sup>rd</sup> full voltammetry cycles within the frequency range of 100 kHz–100 mHz using the amplitude of alternate voltage signal of 10 mV.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06295&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The corresponding Nyquist plots for the electrodes under study are shown in the insets of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a and c. Nova 2.1 analyzed all impedance spectra. The EIS measurements show an electrode/electrolyte interphase, which can be represented by a medium- to high-frequency semicircle at the OCV with a resistance of around 65 Ω for the pristine NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> electrode, which decreases to about 44 Ω after the first CV cycle and then increases to around 51.5 Ω following the third CV cycle (inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). These changes in resistance could be due to microstructural reorganization within the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> electrode, as well as the gradual growth and alteration of the SEI layer. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c (inset) shows an initial low resistance (around 28.4 Ω) for NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C at the OCV, which is then slightly decreased to around 15.0 Ω after the first CV cycle and then increased to around 15.7 Ω after three CV cycles, indicating an improved electronic conductivity. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> reports the results of the fitting of the impedance spectra for the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes in lithium coin cells.</div><div class="NLM_p">In analogy to LIBs, in the first cycle, two reduction potentials of both NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> (0.83 and 0.36 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C (0.93 and 0.40 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) electrodes are observed in SIBs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b and d). The weak reduction potential at about ∼0.90 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na is attributed to Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion into the layers of WS<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> The dominant peak at ∼0.40 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na is caused by the conversion reaction of WS<sub>2</sub> with Na<sup>+</sup> ions resulting in metallic W embedded in an amorphous Na<sub>2</sub>S matrix and the formation of the SEI film. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> The first oxidation of W to WS<sub>2</sub> during the desodiation process is reflected by three oxidation potentials at 1.92, 2.30, and 2.56 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na in the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C (1.91, 2.29, and 2.54 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) electrodes. In the following sweeps, three cathodic peaks at ∼2.00, ∼1.30, and ∼0.90 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na and three anodic peaks at around ∼1.90, ∼2.30, and ∼2.50 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na appear in both electrodes, suggesting the presence of a multistep Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and deintercalation process.</div><div class="NLM_p">The NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes exhibit different tendencies in following cycles, as suggested by EIS (insets in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b and d). The NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> electrode shows a significant change in the electrode/electrolyte interface resistance from a high value (1437 Ω) at the OCV to about 90.5 Ω after the first CV cycle and then to 59.7 Ω at the third CV cycle, which can be extracted from impedance measurements at a high-to-middle frequency (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b, inset). As shown in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d, the interfacial resistance of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode is about 42.3 Ω after one CV cycle and then decreased to approximately 41.2 Ω after three CV cycles, which is lower than 256.5 Ω for the initial resistance determined at the OCV. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> presents the fitting results of the impedance spectra for the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes in sodium coin cells.</div><div class="NLM_p">Given that Na<sup>+</sup> has a larger ionic radius (1.02 Å) than Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å), the transport of Na<sup>+</sup> through the SEI layer and diffusion into the bulk electrode were quite sluggish in comparison to Li<sup>+</sup>. This means that a thicker SEI film formed on the SIBs electrodes than on the LIBs ones. As a result, in Li-ion cells, the electrode has a much lower impedance value than in Na-ion cells. Overall, the WS<sub>2</sub>-carbon matrix effectively improves the kinetic properties of the electrodes in both LIBs and SIBs, which is associated with the excellent electrochemical activity of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> These EIS results and the CV responses give clear evidence of the improvement of the Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion thanks to the carbon coating and layered tubular structure of the electrode material, which enhance the electrode kinetics. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Such optimized charge transfer kinetics of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C may ameliorate the electrochemical properties, resulting in reduced cell polarization and improved cycling ability.</div><div class="NLM_p">CV and EIS measurements indicate that the one-step carbon coating accelerates the electrochemical activity of nanotubular WS<sub>2</sub> by triggering a quick and reversible lithiation/delithiation. Such improvements were further demonstrated by comparative cycling tests of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>, NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C, and commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> electrodes in lithium half-cells at a current density of 80 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a–d display the selected voltage profiles and cycling response. The first inclined discharge plateau of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C are observed at about 0.7 V, leading to discharge capacities of 504 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 684 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a and b). The pristine NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> has an initial Coulombic efficiency of 88.1%, which is higher than that of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C (81.5%) and commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> (85.2%), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d. This is partly ascribed to the conversion reaction of WS<sub>2</sub> to metallic W embedded in a Li<sub>2</sub>S matrix, irreversible electrolyte decomposition, and formation of the SEI film. According to the remarkable hysteresis of conversion materials, the subsequent charge evolves over a broad voltage range, which is distinguished by an oxidation plateau at about 2.2 V, corresponding to the oxidation of Li<sub>2</sub>S to S, with specific capacities of 444 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 528 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C, respectively. In the first lithiation and delithiation process, the same peaks at 0.7 and 2.2 V for commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> are also found, delivering reversible capacities of 512 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 436 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c). In fact, a slight participation of carbon to the lithium exchange <i>via</i> low-voltage insertion and a certain extent of interfacial Li<sup>+</sup> storage at the W/Li<sub>2</sub>S interface could partially explain the relatively high reversible capacities of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>, NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C, and commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/medium/am2c06295_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a–d) Cycling performance of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>, NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C, and commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> in lithium coin cells galvanostatically cycled at a current density 80 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in terms of (a–c) voltage profiles and (d) specific capacity and Coulombic efficiency (right <i>Y</i>-axis) vs cycle number. (e and f) Rate capacity of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C in a lithium coin cell galvanostatically cycled at current densities of 80, 120, 200, 300, 400, and 600 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in terms of (e) voltage profiles and (f) specific capacity vs cycle number. (g) Cycling performance of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C in a lithium coin cell galvanostatically cycled at a high current density of 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Voltage range: 0.01–3.0 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06295&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d shows that commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> fails fast during cycling, while NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C have much higher capacities  and better Coulombic efficiencies. At the 60<sup>th</sup> cycle, the capacity of commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> drops to roughly 147 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Pristine NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> undergoes a rapid capacity fading down to around 157 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 60 cycles, whereas NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C has a much more stable cycling stability, with a specific capacity of 305 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 60<sup>th</sup> cycle. The higher capacity of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> than commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> could be ascribed to the nanotube structure of WS<sub>2</sub>, which provides shorter diffusion paths and a higher population of active sites for Li<sup>+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> The 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> flakes have possibly larger crystallites than the WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes, also resulting in longer diffusion lengths for Li<sup>+</sup>. Moreover, the interlayer distance in nanotubes is larger, which enables easier movement of Li<sup>+</sup> . <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The carbon coating in the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C nanocomposites deterred polysulfide dissolution, which helped to boost capacity retention. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The carbon coating acted as an elastic buffer, allowing the electrode to accommodate large volume changes during cycling, resulting in good cycle stability for the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode. Some capacity loss in the three electrodes could be caused by mechanical degradation, such as loss of electrical contact and active material. Furthermore, similar to lithium–sulfur batteries, polysulfide dissolution in the electrolyte could account for some of the capacity loss. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The rate capability of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode was then assessed by cycling it at current densities ranging from 80 to 600 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e and f presents the selected voltage profiles and specific capacities. The NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode reveals a slight polarization as the current density is increased stepwise to 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which shows a moderate degree polarization until 600 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The specific capacity decreases as the current density is increased for every 10 cycles, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e. The NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode delivers reversible capacities of about 497, 456, 419, 373, 307, and 209 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the current densities 80, 160, 200, 300, 400, and 600 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f). As the current is turned back to 80 mA g<sup>–1</sup> at the 62<sup>nd</sup> cycle, the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode exhibits a discharge capacity of around 321 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 70<sup>th</sup> cycle. This promising rate capability suggests that the carbon coating (around 5 wt %) could enhance the electronic/ionic transport within the electrode, allowing for better electrochemical kinetics. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, the long cycle performance of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode was tested at a high current density of 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g). The initial reversible capacity of the electrode in LIBs can reach around 485 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, then gradually decreases to 146 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the 100<sup>th</sup> cycle, and slightly declines to 103 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a Coulombic efficiency of nearly 99.5% after 200 cycles, indicating its good cycling stability.</div><div class="NLM_p">In terms of morphological features and particle size, the work published in <i>Chemistry─A European Journal</i> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref59" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> shows that WS<sub>2</sub> has nanowire morphology (WS<sub>2</sub> NWs) with a quite thin diameter of ∼25 nm. However, the particle size of the active material is too small, resulting in a low compaction density of the electrode material and a reduction in energy density.
