On the other hand, the work published in <i>Carbon</i> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> states that a graphene-doped self-assembled WS<sub>2</sub> nanohoneycomb (WS<sub>2</sub>/rGO Nano-HC) composite has nanoplanar structure. As we mentioned before, the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C nanocomposites used in this study have nanostructure properties (a large specific surface area, good electrical conductivity, and desired highly active sites) as well as tubular shape with diameter distributions in the range 50–200 nm. According to the carbon coating approach, WS<sub>2</sub> NWs published in <i>Chemistry─A European Journal</i> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref59" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> were prepared <i>via</i> a simple solvothermal process followed by a thermal treatment. The WS<sub>2</sub>/rGO Nano-HC composite published in <i>Carbon</i> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> was prepared through an improved one-step hydrothermal method. However, the solvothermal or hydrothermal reaction has the following drawbacks: it is unsuitable for mass production and has a low level of safety. Our simple, fast, and inexpensive production method involves precipitating sucrose precursor on WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes and then pyrolyzing it to form NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C nanocomposites. What distinguishes our synthetic approach presented herein from previously reported ones is that these nanotubes have closed cage structure, which provides them with chemical stability and restricts volume change. From the point of view of uniformity, the EDS mapping of the WS<sub>2</sub> NWs published in <i>Chemistry─A European Journal</i> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref59" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> illustrates a homogeneous dispersion of W and S along the nanowires. As a result, WS<sub>2</sub> NWs display an extremely long cycle life while also having a high capacity and rate capability. According to the TEM elemental EDS mapping results in the paper of <i>Carbon</i>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref60" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> graphene accounts for roughly 14% of the weight percentage. Because of its high strength, graphene enhances the mechanical characteristics of nanostructures as well as the specific charge capacity, cycling life, and high charge/discharge rates of as-prepared batteries. The results of SEM-EDS and HAADF-STEM-EDS in our work demonstrate that the W and S elements are uniformly distributed within the WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes, with the C layer located on the nanotube surface (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>j). Low carbon content (around 5%), as revealed by combining TGA, SEM-EDS, and HAADF-STEM-EDS results, leads to high practical capacity, satisfactory cycling stability, and suitable rate capability for use in both LIBs and SIBs. Overall, even though the cycling performance of our electrode material (NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C) is not as stable as the works reported in the literature, we highlight that the chemical stability, the robust mechanical properties, the low carbon content, together with the facile and cost-effective production method of the present nanostructures render them as an “interesting player” in the field of LIBs and SIBs. Accordingly, our results indicate that the synthesis pathway plays a significant role in improving the electrochemical properties of metal sulfide nanomaterials through strengthening the electric contact within the electrode. The enhanced charge transfer kinetics of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode indicates its applicability as an anode material for LIBs and SIBs. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a> presents the electrochemical properties of several recently reported WS<sub>2</sub>-based anode materials for LIBs and SIBs, allowing a facile visual comparison. The cycling stability of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C nanocomposites in our work is superior to that reported for recently published materials, such as Co-doped WS<sub>2</sub> (380 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 50 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 40 cycles in LIBs) and WS<sub>2</sub> nanoplates (158 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles in SIBs). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57 ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57 ref58" aria-label="reference 59 and 60">(59,60)</a></div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a> depicts the selected voltage profiles and capacity trends of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>, NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C, and commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> electrodes in sodium half-cells at a 80 mA g<sup>–1</sup> current density. The three short plateaus at about 1.18–1.06 V, 0.62–0.52 V, and 0.32–0.18 V in the first discharge curves of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C are attributed to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into WS<sub>2</sub>, the WS<sub>2</sub> conversion into W nanoparticles and Na<sub>2</sub>S, as well as the formation of the SEI film (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a and b). There are three plateaus in the first charge process: a major plateau at 1.66–1.86 V and two small plateaus at 2.26 and 2.55 V, which should be directly related to the conversion reaction of W into WS<sub>2</sub>. These sodiation and desodiation behaviors are consistent with the CV curves, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d. In comparison, the sodium intercalation platforms of commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> are 0.75 V, 0.47 V, and 0.21–0.16 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c), being significantly lower than those of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C. This shows that the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes have much lower energy barriers for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation. Similar to the cycling performance of LIBs, the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode of SIBs outperforms the pristine NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and commercial bulk 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> electrodes. The practical capacity of SIBs in the same NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> electrode is found to be lower than the corresponding LIBs, which could be directly associated to the slow electrode kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>. The initial discharge and charge capacities of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> electrode are 540 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 407 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, yielding a Coulombic efficiency of 75.4% (calculated from the data in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a). The first discharge and charge capacities of the commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> electrode are 499 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 373 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, leading to a Coulombic efficiency of 74.7% (calculated from the data in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c). This clearly shows that the few-layered WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes and suitable interlayered distance improve the kinetic characteristics of the electrode. The NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode demonstrates higher initial discharge and charge capacities (605 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 465 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively) with an initial Coulombic efficiency of 76.8% (calculated from the data in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b), which is higher than those of pristine NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> (75.4%) and commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> (74.7%). As stated in the section on LIBs performance, such an irreversible capacity may be directly attributable to the irreversible reaction of electrolyte decomposition and the formation of the SEI film. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Therefore, pristine NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> shows a rapid capacity decline, with a reversible capacity of approximately 57 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 60 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d), whereas NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C shows a significantly more stable behavior (a reversible capacity of 152 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the 60<sup>th</sup> cycle). This improved cycling stability might be ascribed to the volume confinement effect of the carbon matrix that tolerates the volume variations during cycling. Meanwhile, the specific capacity of commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> is also very limited, decreasing to 52 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the 60<sup>th</sup> cycle.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/medium/am2c06295_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a–d) Cycling performance of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>, NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C, and commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> in sodium coin cells galvanostatically cycled at a current density 80 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in terms of (a–c) voltage profiles and (d) specific capacity vs cycle number. (e and f) Rate capacity of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C in a sodium coin cell galvanostatically cycled at current densities of 80, 120, 200, 300, 400, and 600 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in terms of (e) voltage profiles and (f) specific capacity vs cycle number. (g) Cycling performance of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C in a sodium coin cell galvanostatically cycled at a high current density of 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Voltage range: 0.01–3.0 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06295&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The rate capability of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode was then investigated by cycling at current densities ranging from 80, 160, 200, 300, 400, to 600 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e and f). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>f shows a delivered capacity of approximately 458 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 80 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which decreases to about 272 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> once the current density is increased to 300 mA g<sup>–1</sup> due to an expected slight increase in polarization (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e). A further increase in current density to 600 mA g<sup>–1</sup> reduces the reversible capacity to 115 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, as the current density decreases back to the initial value of 80 mA g<sup>–1</sup> at the 62<sup>nd</sup> cycle, the cell exhibits a discharge capacity of about 318 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, but still the capacity quickly fades because of electrode degradation.</div><div class="NLM_p">The cycling performance of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode in SIBs at a high current density of 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup> was further investigated, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>g. The initial reversible capacity of the electrode is about 321 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and then speedily decreases to 59 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the 100<sup>th</sup> cycle and progressively decreases to 29 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the 200<sup>th</sup> cycle. LIBs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g) clearly outperform SIBs in capacity performance at the high current density because the larger diameter of the Na<sup>+</sup> ion makes embedding more difficult and causes more damage to electrode materials.</div><div class="NLM_p">The NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode demonstrates good cycling performance and rate capability in both LIBs and SIBs, which is thought to be due to the synergistic effect between the tubular architecture of WS<sub>2</sub> and the advanced electronic conduction pathways contributed by carbon. These findings imply that the synthesis pathway used herein enables the development of an improved NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode that could be employed successfully as the anode in LIBs and SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">To emphasize the optimized electrochemical properties of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C nanocomposites, the morphology features of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes after 60 cycles at 80 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in lithium and sodium coin cells were investigated by SEM, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a–d. The NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> electrode material shows obvious agglomeration in LIBs, as demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a, and the structure was impaired. After 60 cycles in SIBs, WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes were severely crushed and shed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b). This morphology change is due to the fact that the nanotube structure cannot effectively mitigate volume expansion, resulting in poor capacity retention (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>).</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/medium/am2c06295_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a–d) SEM images of the (a and b) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and (c and d) NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes after 60 cycles in lithium coin cells and sodium coin cells, respectively. (e) XRD patterns and (f) Raman spectra of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode before and after 60 cycles in a lithium coin cell and in a sodium coin cell.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06295&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">On the contrary, as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>c and d, the nanotube morphology of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes remains intact, further supporting the claim that the WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes are protected by carbon coating. The carbon covering efficiently prevented NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> morphological change while allowing the formation of the stable SEI film in both LIBs and SIBs. However, as the carbon covering thinned during cycling, it might potentially obstruct Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and deintercalationas well as the SEI formation, resulting in the deterioration of cycling performance, as revealed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further determine the phase change during cycling, the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes before and after cycling at 80 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in LIBs and SIBs were investigated by XRD and Raman. The XRD graphs of the cycled electrodes reveal a transition of WS<sub>2</sub> from crystalline to amorphous, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>e. The 2θ peaks at 42.7°, 49.8°, and 73.4° correlate to the peaks of the Cu foil. The peak of the (002), (103), (006), and (114) WS<sub>2</sub> crystal planes in both lithium and sodium coin cells stays consistent, denoting that Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> are preferentially inserted into certain crystallographic planes. The peaks of the (110) and (101) crystal planes vanish in both LIBs and SIBs, implying that preferential intercalation and deintercalation of Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> damage the structure of specific crystal planes. It is interesting to note the preservation of WS<sub>2</sub> stoichiometry with prevalence after the 60<sup>th</sup> charging/discharging cycle. As a result, it is reasonable to assume that Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and deintercalation in NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C are likely to alter the structure of some crystal planes, and thus, certain peaks will no longer be present in the XRD diffractogram. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>f, the distinctive peaks of the D-band and G-band (at roughly 1354 and 1600 cm<sup>–1</sup>), which correspond to the sucrose-derived carbon, are located at almost the same positions in all samples after 60 charging/discharging cycles. At the same time, the D-band and G-band peaks of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes in LIBs show weaker intensity than in SIBs, which may be attributed to the faster electrode kinetics of Li<sup>+</sup> than Na<sup>+</sup>. The characteristic E<sub>2g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> peaks (at about 351 and 419 cm<sup>–1</sup>) become indistinguishable in all specimens after cycling in both LIBs and SIBs, reflecting a structural transformation from crystalline to amorphous structure.</div><div class="NLM_p">To obtain a better overall view of the electrochemical behavior, the morphology features of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes before and after 60 cycles at 80 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in lithium and sodium coin cells were examined by TEM, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>. TEM and HRTEM images of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S2a and b</a>) and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>a and b) electrodes before cycling show the original 1D nanotube morphology, with visible lattice fringes. The SAED pattern of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2c</a> demonstrates rather clear and bright diffraction spots. When the SAED pattern was taken from the fresh NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> with C before cycling (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>c), the amount of carbon is too small, which still shows strong diffraction spots.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/medium/am2c06295_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a) TEM image, (b) HRTEM image, and (c) SAED pattern of the fresh NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode. (d–i) TEM images, HRTEM images, and SAED patterns of (d–f) the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode after 60 cycles in a lithium coin cell and (g–i) the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode after 60 cycles in a sodium coin cell.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/asset/images/large/am2c06295_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c06295&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">After being charge–discharged for 60 cycles in LIBs, the TEM and HRTEM images of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2d and e</a>) and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>d and e) electrodes demonstrate the 1D nanotube morphology, but with no apparent lattice fringes. In fact, NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C transform to amorphous structure as further indicated by the SAED patterns in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2f</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>f. These diffraction spots are substantially weaker than those before cycling, indicating that the nanotube structure has been largely broken.</div><div class="NLM_p">The 1D nanotube morphologies of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes after 60 cycles in SIBs were preserved, as depicted in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2g</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>g. The HRTEM images of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes after 60 cycles in SIBs show no visible lattice fringes, as displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2h</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>h. Furthermore, the SAED patterns in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2i</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>i suggest that the diffraction spots are significantly weaker after cycling, indicating that the crystallinity degree of the nanotube was modified and highly crystalline NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C were converted from strong diffraction to amorphous materials during cycling.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Interestingly, the morphologies of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode materials before and after cycling are still 1D nanotubes but with numerous structural changes. The lattice fringes of the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrode in both LIBs and SIBs after cycling cannot be specifically identified, which is probably because the structures experienced some volume expansion during cycling.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C nanocomposites were prepared by annealing WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes together with sucrose in an inert gas atmosphere. The resulting NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C material consists of nanotubes with increased (compared to bulk 2H-WS<sub>2</sub>) interlayer distances that create porous structures in their intersections; these structures promote electrode electrochemical activity by suppressing side reactions between the electrolyte and electrode. Furthermore, the conductive carbon coating offers effective electronic transfer routes as well as fast Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, resulting in enhanced electrochemical performance. Notably, the NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C electrodes deliver comparatively high discharge capacities of 305 and 152 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 60 cycles for LIBs and SIBs, respectively; moreover, the cycling trend is relatively stable and the rate capability is satisfactory. Ultimately, the simple, fast, and inexpensive synthesis proposed appears to be an advantageous and scalable pathway for producing high-capacity conversion metal sulfides for rechargeable battery applications.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Chemicals</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Carbon black (CB, Cabot Corporation); poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF, Alfa Aesar); <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolidone (NMP, 99.7%, Sigma-Aldrich); commercial tungsten disulfide (99%, Aldrich); ethanol (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>OH, 99.8%) were bought from Penta, Czech Republic, and used without further purification. Nitrogen gas (99.9999%) was obtained from SIAD. A solution of lithium hexafluorophosphate (LiPF<sub>6</sub>) in ethylene carbonate (EC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC) was bought from Sigma-Aldrich: that is, 1.0 M LiPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC (1/1, v/v), battery grade. A solution of sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) in ethylene carbonate (EC), dimethyl carbonate (DMC), and ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC) with 5.0% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC, stabilizer) was purchased from Suzhou Fosai New material Co., Ltd.: that, is 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC/EMC (1/1/1, v/v/v) with 5.0% FEC, battery grade.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Synthesis of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p last">The WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes were synthesized using a multistep but one-pot reaction route, at high-temperature in a custom-designed quartz reactor embedded in a horizontal tube furnace, as described previously. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> WO<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles were reacted with H<sub>2</sub>S/H<sub>2</sub> gases at 840 °C for 3.5 h under N<sub>2</sub> carrier gas flow. This route includes the growth of tungsten suboxide nanowhiskers and their following sulfurization into WS<sub>2</sub> hollow nanotubes.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Synthesis of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C Nanocomposites</h3><div class="NLM_p last">WS<sub>2</sub> nanotubes (0.16 g) were suspended in a solution of water/ethanol (40 mL, 1:3, v/v). 0.04 g of sucrose was added, and the solvent was evaporated while stirring at 70 °C. Wet samples were dried for 2 h in a vacuum oven at 60 °C. The dry mixture was then annealed in an argon atmosphere at 550 °C for 8 h to form NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C nanocomposites. The heating took place in a tube furnace (OTF-1200X-S) with a 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> heating rate and a subsequent step of natural cooling. The NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C samples were ground manually and gently for 2–4 minutes with an agate mortar, yielding a fine black powder.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">XRD characterization was conducted on the samples (NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C) to study their crystalline structure and phase composition. XRD patterns were obtained using a Bruker D8 Advance diffractometer (Cu Kα radiation, λ = 0.154 nm, Germany) over the scattering angle 2θ range of 5°–90° with a scanning step size of 0.02°. Τhe obtained diffractograms were analyzed using the High Score Plus 3.0 software. To examine the morphology of the samples, SEM, TEM, and HRTEM images were recorded with a Tescan MAIA 3 (Czech Republic) and a JEOL 2200 FS microscope (Japan), respectively. The elemental composition of the samples was analyzed through SEM-energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (SEM-EDS) <i>via</i> the X-ACT Oxford Instrument analyzer (England) integrated at the same SEM microscope. It was studied in addition <i>via</i> high-angle annular dark field-STEM-energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (HAADF-STEM-EDS) <i>via</i> the same TEM microscope which was used for TEM imaging. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was performed on a Setaram Themys system to estimate the contents of carbon and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>. The heating rate was 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup>, and the carrier gas (Ar/O<sub>2</sub> 4:1) flow rate was 80 mL min<sup>–1</sup>. XPS was utilized to identify the chemical compositions (Omicron Nanotechnology Ltd., Germany) of the samples using a monochromatic Al X-ray radiation source (Kα1 = 1486.7 eV). Raman spectroscopy (532 nm laser, Renishaw, UK) was employed to confirm the vibration modes of the species present on the surface of the samples. Nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms were measured through a NOVAtouch 2200 instrument (Quantachrome, USA). Before the sorption trials, the samples were outgassed for 4 h at 150 °C under high vacuum (0.05 Pa). The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method was utilized to calculate the specific surface areas of the samples. The (Barrett–Joyner–Halenda) BJH algorithm was used to extract pore size distributions from isotherms.</div></div><div id="sec4_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Battery Assembly and Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrode slurry was made up of 80% active material (NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>, NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C, and commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub>), 10% CB conducting agent, and 10% PVDF binder, with NMP serving as solvent. The homogenized slurry was coated onto a copper foil current collector (thickness of 10 μm) <i>via</i> a laboratory doctor blade. The slurry was then dried for 3 h at 70 °C in an oven before being cut into a round shape with a diameter of 15 mm and dried completely under vacuum at 110 °C overnight. The mass loadings of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>, NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C, and commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub> over the electrodes were 0.9–1.3 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Electrochemical evaluation of the electrodes was performed using two-electrode coin cells (CR2032; MTI Corporation) that were assembled by metallic Li or Na foils together with an electrode disk. CR2032 coin cells were separated by a glass microfiber film (Whatman, grade GF/D) soaked by two different electrolytes (battery grade, BASF). For the half-cell in LIBs, 1.0 M LiPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC (1/1, v/v) was used as the electrolyte. For the half-cell in SIBs, 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC/EMC (1/1/1, v/v/v) with 5.0% FEC was used as the electrolyte. These cells were prepared in a glovebox filled with argon (MBRAUN, Germany; O<sub>2</sub> content &lt;0.1 ppm, H<sub>2</sub>O content &lt;0.1 ppm).</div><div class="NLM_p">CV coupled with EIS tests were conducted on the same coin cell employing an Autolab PGSTAT204 workstation (Eco Chemie, Utrecht, Netherlands). CV measurements on the electrodes (NTs-WS<sub>2</sub> and NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C) were carried out at a scanning rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the working voltage window of 0.01 to 3.0 V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li. Under different conditions (open circuit voltage, OCV; after the first and third voltammetry cycles), an EIS test was recorded using an AC voltage (10 mV amplitude) in the 100 kHz to 100 mHz frequency range. The spectra were analyzed through the Nova 2.1 software and then fit using the Kronig−Kramers test.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The cycling behavior and rate capability of the electrodes (NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>, NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C, and commercial 2H-WS<sub>2</sub>) were explored in CR2032 coin cells at various current densities in the voltage range 0.01–3.0 V. Cycling tests were carried out at current densities of 80 and 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. In terms of the rate performance of NTs-WS<sub>2</sub>@C, the cells were cycled at current densities ranging from 80 to 120, 200, 300, 400, and 600 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (total 10 cycles for each current density). The galvanostatic cycling measurements were all conducted at room temperature by means of a Neware battery test system (BTX 7.6, Shenzhen, China).</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-3"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c06295?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c06295</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Tables summarizing the fitting results of the impedance spectra and summary of the electrochemical performance of published WS<sub>2</sub>-based anodes for LIBs and SIBs. TEM images and corresponding SAED patterns of NTs-WS2@C before and after cycling. (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf">am2c06295_si_001.pdf (424.94 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.2c06295" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable hydrogen gas batteries show promises for the integration of renewable yet intermittent solar and wind electricity into the grid energy storage.  Here, we describe a rechargeable, high-rate, and long-life hydrogen gas battery that exploits a nanostructured lithium manganese oxide cathode and a hydrogen gas anode in an aq. electrolyte.  The proposed lithium manganese oxide-hydrogen battery shows a discharge potential of ~ 1.3 V, a remarkable rate of 50 C with Coulombic efficiency of ~ 99.8%, and a robust cycle life.  A systematic electrochem. study demonstrates the significance of the electrocatalytic hydrogen gas anode and reveals the charge storage mechanism of the lithium manganese oxide-hydrogen battery.  This work provides opportunities for the development of new rechargeable hydrogen batteries for the future grid-scale energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/adma.202110631"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Crystalline-Amorphous Interfaces Coupling of CoSe<sub>2</sub>/CoP with Optimized D-Band Center and Boosted Electrocatalytic Hydrogen Evolution</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Crystalline-Amorphous Interfaces Coupling of CoSe2/CoP with Optimized d-Band Center and Boosted Electrocatalytic Hydrogen Evolution</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shen, Shijie; Wang, Zongpeng; Lin, Zhiping; Song, Kai; Zhang, Qinghua; Meng, Fanqi; Gu, Lin; Zhong, Wenwu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Amorphous and heterojunction materials have been widely used in the field of electrocatalytic hydrogen evolution due to their unique physicochem. properties.  However, the current used individual strategy still has limited effects.  Hence efficient tailoring tactics with synergistic effect are highly desired.  Herein, the authors have realized the deep optimization of catalytic activity by a constructing cryst.-amorphous CoSe2/CoP heterojunction.  Benefiting from the strong electronic coupling at the interfaces, the d-band center of the material moves further down compared to its cryst.-cryst. counterpart, optimizing the valence state and the H adsorption of Co and lowering the kinetic barrier of hydrogen evolution reaction (HER).  The heterojunction shows an overpotential of 65 mV to drive a c.d. of 10 mA cm-2 in the acidic medium.  Besides, it also shows competitive properties in both neutral and basic media.  This work provides inspiration for optimizing the catalytic activity through combining a cryst. and amorphous heterojunction, which can be implemented for other transition metal compd. electrocatalysts.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202200855"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Realizing Negatively Charged Metal Atoms through Controllable D-Electron Transfer in Ternary Ir<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>Rh<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb Intermetallic Alloy for Hydrogen Evolution Reaction</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2200855</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Realizing Negatively Charged Metal Atoms through Controllable d-Electron Transfer in Ternary Ir1-xRhxSb Intermetallic Alloy for Hydrogen Evolution Reaction</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lin, Zhiping; Xiao, BeiBei; Huang, Min; Yan, Linghui; Wang, Zongpeng; Huang, Yucong; Shen, Shijie; Zhang, Qinghua; Gu, Lin; Zhong, Wenwu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">25</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2200855</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Alloying noble metals with non-noble metals is a promising method to fabricate catalysts, with the advantages of reduced noble metal usage and excellent activity.  In this work, electron-abundant Ir/Rh sites, as highly active centers for the hydrogen evolution reaction (HER), are realized by fabricating Ir1-xRhxSb alloys through the arc-melting method.  The electron transfer from Sb to Ir/Rh makes the latter neg. charged, leading to considerably optimized adsorption for active H species during HER.  As a result, the Ir1-xRhxSb alloy exhibits outstanding activity for HER, with an optimized overpotential of 22 mV at 10 mA cm-2 and a Tafel slope of 47.6 mV dec-1.  This work provides insights into highly active alloys and sheds light on the utilization of electron-abundant metal atoms.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.rser.2018.02.007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Notton, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nivet, M.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Voyant, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Paoli, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Darras, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Motte, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fouilloy, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Intermittent and Stochastic Character of Renewable Energy Sources: Consequences, Cost of Intermittence and Benefit of Forecasting</span>. <i>Renew. K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-Ion Batteries: Present and Future</span>. <i>Chem. Soc. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>46</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3529</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3614</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C6CS00776G</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D46%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D3529-3614%26journal%3DChem.%2BSoc.%2BRev.%26author%3DJ.%2BY.%2BHwang%26author%3DS.%2BT.%2BMyung%26author%3DY.%2BK.%2BSun%26title%3DSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BPresent%2Band%2BFuture%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC6CS00776G&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c06295&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC6CS00776G&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201909530"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Cathode Choice for Commercialization of Sodium-Ion Batteries: Layered Transition Metal Oxides versus Prussian Blue Analogs</span>. <i>Adv. With the unprecedentedly increasing demand for renewable and clean energy sources, the sodium-ion battery (SIB) is emerging as an alternative or complementary energy storage candidate to the present com. lithium-ion battery due to the abundance and low cost of sodium resources.  Layered transition metal oxides and Prussian blue analogs are reviewed in terms of their com. potential as cathode materials for SIBs.  The recent progress in research on their half cells and full cells for the ultimate application in SIBs are summarized.  In addn., their electrochem. performance, suitability for scaling up, cost, and environmental concerns are compared in detail with a brief outlook on future prospects.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tailoring the pore size of a polypropylene separator with a polymer having intrinsic nanoporosity for suppressing the polysulfide shuttle in lithium-sulfur batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yu, Xingwen; Wu, Hao; Koo, Joseph H.; Manthiram, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-sulfur (Li-S) batteries are being considered as one of the most promising candidates for the development of next-generation energy storage technologies.  Although much progress has been made over the past decade, the development of Li-S batteries is still held back by a crucial polysulfide-shuttle problem.  To address this crit. issue, an approach to reduce the pore size of the separator is presented here, to prevent the penetration of sol. polysulfide species.  A polymer with intrinsic nanoporosity (PIN) is developed within the micrometer-scale pores of a polypropylene separator.  The framework of polypropylene acts as a skeleton to sustain reliable mech. properties with the thin membrane.  Upon the formation of PIN in the pores, the polypropylene separator maintains its thickness.  With the thin PIN-polypropylene membrane, the Li-S cells can be operated with a relatively high sulfur loading.  The PIN allows the transport of Li+ ions, but suppresses the penetration of the polysulfide species.  The Li-S batteries with the PIN-modified polypropylene separator exhibit enhanced cycling performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201902485"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bresser, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Transition Metal Oxide Anodes for Electrochemical Energy Storage in Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) with outstanding energy and power d. have been extensively investigated in recent years, rendering them the most suitable energy storage technol. for application in emerging markets such as elec. vehicles and stationary storage.  More recently, sodium, one of the most abundant elements on earth, exhibiting similar physicochem. properties as lithium, has been gaining increasing attention for the development of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) in order to address the concern about Li availability and cost-esp. with regard to stationary applications for which size and vol. of the battery are of less importance.  Compared with traditional intercalation reactions, conversion reaction-based transition metal oxides (TMOs) are prospective anode materials for rechargeable batteries thanks to their low cost and high gravimetric specific capacities.  In this review, the recent progress and remaining challenges of conversion reactions for LIBs and SIBs are discussed, covering an overview about the different synthesis methods, morphol. characteristics, as well as their electrochem. performance.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">MoS2/N-doped graphene aerogels composite anode for high performance sodium/potassium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dong, Xiaoyu; Xing, Zheng; Zheng, Guojun; Gao, Xinran; Hong, Haiping; Ju, Zhicheng; Zhuang, Quanchao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">339</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">135932</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Three-dimensional porous MoS2/N-doped graphene aerogels (MoS2/NGA) with different MoS2 loading masses were synthesized by a simple hydrothermal method.  The unique structure can provide more Na+ (K+) transport channels and the three-dimensional porous morphol. could greatly absorb the vol. expansion of MoS2 during charging and discharging process.  As the anode of Na-ion batteries (SIBs), this material provides a specific capacity of 673 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 with long cycle stability.  The composite shows a specific capacity of 305 mAh g-1 at 2000 mA g-1 and exhibits excellent capacity reversibility after rate performance test, indicating that it is a promising anode choice for SIB.  As the anode material of K-ion batteries (KIBs), it provides a reversible specific capacity of 349 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1.  It has a specific capacity of 72 mAhg-1 at 5000 mAg-1 and exhibits excellent capacity reversibility after rate performance test as well.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Siloxene: A potential layered silicon intercalation anode for Na, Li and K ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Loaiza, Laura C.; Monconduit, Laure; Seznec, Vincent</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The alloying reaction between 14th group elements and alkali can be detrimental for Li, Na or K-ion batteries due to the large assocd. vol. expansion that leads to a rapid capacity fading.  In order to overcome this issue, their assocd. 2D phases are promising anodes that enable alkali intercalation without alloying reaction and vol. expansion.  In this study, the lamellar siloxene obtained from topotactic deintercalation of Ca from CaSi2 delivered reversible capacities of 2300, 311 and 203 mAh/g for Li, Na and K, resp., with good capacity retention and coulombic efficiency for Li and Na.  The intercalation mechanism taking place upon cycling is highlighted on the basis of ex situ Raman characterization combined with IR spectroscopy, SEM and TEM.  To the best of our knowledge, it is the first time that a lamellar Silicon based material shows such high stable capacity without vol. expansion, representing a real breakthrough for the batteries field and particularly for NIB.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1016/j.jallcom.2018.10.371"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lian, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Razzaq, A. Rechargeable sodium/potassium-ion batteries (SIBs/PIBs) with abundant reserves of Na/K and low cost have been a promising substitution to com. lithium-ion batteries.  As for pivotal anode materials, metal sulfides (MSx) exhibit an inspiring potential due to the multitudinous redox storage mechanisms for SIBs/PIBs applications.  Nevertheless, they still confront several bottlenecks, such as the low elec. cond., poor ionic diffusivity, sluggish interfacial/surface reaction kinetics, and severe vol. expansion, which distinctly restrain the battery performance.  Meanwhile, the systematic insights into the design strategies of MSx for SIBs/PIBs have been seldom elaborated.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Abstr.: Lithium-ion batteries have aided the portable electronics revolution for nearly three decades. Basic science research, involving solid-state chem. and physics, has been at the center of this endeavor, particularly during the 1970s and 1980s.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The effects of modifications in the synthesis Li/Co/dopant concns. on the performance and cycle life of Li Co oxide were studied to learn how different modification methods work in relation to each other and to provide data for up-to-date com. interest.  The LiCoO2 materials were prepd. using the same precursors and synthesis process to ensure the comparability.  The electrochem. characterizations were performed in both half-cells and LiCoO2/graphite pouch cells.  The Mg-Ti doped LiCoO2 shows superior performance compared to stoichiometric and over-lithiated LiCoO2.  The Mg-Ti doped sample shows 89% capacity retention after 1000 cycles in 3.0-4.2 V and 80% capacity retention after 240 cycles in 3.0-4.4 V in LiCoO2/graphite pouch cell.  The better rate capability is attributed to Ti doping reducing the Co valence in LiCoO2, making it more metallic and conductive.  The longer cycle life of the doped LiCoO2, in turn, is attributed to a better structural stability caused mainly by Mg doping.  This is also reflected in a smaller increase in the charge transfer impedance during cycling.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Characterization of silicon- and carbon-based composite anodes for lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Khomenko, Volodymyr G.; Barsukov, Viacheslav Z.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Anode-active materials with higher reversible capacity than that of conventional graphite have been studied and the electrochem. performance of some C/Si-based materials was analyzed.  Various Si-based composites were prepd. using graphite, hard C and graphitized C black.  An anal. of charging-discharging processes at electrodes based on different C materials showed that graphite modified with Si is the most promising anode material.  The irreversible capacity mainly depended on the Si content.  An optimum content of Si was detd. by taking into account that a high irreversible capacity is not suitable for Li-ion batteries. The small irreversible capacity of the material allowed developing full Li-ion rechargeable cells in the 2016 coin cell configuration. With the fast development of nanoscience and nanotechnol., various structures and materials probably overcome the deficiencies of both devices to improve their electrochem. performance further.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Comprehensive Analysis of the Interphasial and Structural Evolution over Long-Term Cycling of Ultrahigh-Nickel Cathodes in Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Jianyu; Manthiram, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ultrahigh-Ni layered oxides hold great promise as high-energy-d. cathodes at an affordable cost for lithium-ion batteries, yet their practical application is greatly hampered by the poor cyclability.  Herein, by employing LiNi0.94Co0.06O2 as a model cathode in a full-cell configuration, the interphasial and structural evolution processes of ultrahigh-Ni layered oxides are systematically investigated over the course of their service life (1500 cycles).  By applying advanced analytic techniques (e.g., Li-isotope labeling, region-of-interest method), the dynamic chem. evolution on the cathode surface is revealed with spatial resoln., and the correlation between lattice distortion and cathode surface reactivity is established.  Benefiting from in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) anal., the ultrahigh-Ni layered oxide is demonstrated to undergo dual-phase reaction mechanisms with huge lattice variation, which leads to a decrease in crystallinity and secondary particle pulverization.  Furthermore, the crit. impact of cathode surface reaction on the graphite anode-electrolyte interphase (AEI) is revealed at nanometer scale, and a universal chem./phys. evolution process of the AEI is illustrated, for the first time.  Finally, the practical viability of ultrahigh-Ni layered oxides is demonstrated through Al-doping strategy.  This work presents a comprehensive understanding of the structural and interphasial degrdn. of ultrahigh-Ni layered oxide cathodes for developing high-energy-d. lithium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1002/smtd.201900648"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chua, D.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The Advances of Metal Sulfides and In Situ Characterization Methods beyond Li Ion Batteries: Sodium, Potassium, and Aluminum Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Jingwei; Chua, Daniel H. C.; Lee, Pooi See</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small Methods</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With increasing cost and supply risks of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), exploitation of alternative metal-ion batteries has been ongoing in recent years.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), potassium-ion batteries (PIBs), and aluminum-ion batteries (AIBs) have unique advantages as post-LIB alternatives.  Nonetheless, development of electrode materials with high capacity, good reversibility, and extended cycling stability remains challenging.  Metal sulfides (MSs), with improved electronic cond. and better reversibility than metal oxide counterparts, as well as high theor. capacities, are deemed as promising electrode materials and have been investigated in SIBs, PIBs, and AIBs.  However, the cycling stability of MSs is still a persistent limitation.  In order to manifest the advantages while overcoming the drawbacks of MSs, multiple strategies have been attempted.  In this review, the approaches to improve the electrochem. performances of MSs in SIBs, PIBs, and AIBs, including nanostructuring and carbon incorporation, creation of hollow interiors, compn. and crystal structures engineering, etc., are summarized and elaborated.  Recent advances using in situ characterization methods to probe the complex electrochem. mechanisms of MSs in SIBs, PIBs, and AIBs are also presented and highlighted.  The issues to be tackled are listed and the future directions for performance improvements of MSs in SIBs, PIBs, and AIBs are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref26" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1002/ijch.201400124"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Woo, S.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fullerene-like Re-Doped MoS2 Nanoparticles as an Intercalation Host with Fast Kinetics for Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Woo, Seung Hee; Yadgarov, Lena; Rosentsveig, Rita; Park, Yuwon; Song, Daesun; Tenne, Reshef; Hong, Sung You</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Israel Journal of Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">55</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">599-603</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ISJCAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0021-2148</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as a promising alternative to threaten the reign of lithium ion batteries (LIBs) among various next-generation rechargeable energy storage systems, including magnesium ion, metal air, and metal sulfur batteries.  Since both sodium and lithium are located in Group 1 of the periodic table, they share similar (electro)chem. properties with regard to ionization pattern, electronegativity, and electronic configuration; thus the vast no. of compds. developed from LIBs can provide guidance to design electrode materials for SIBs.  However, the larger ionic radius of the sodium cation and unique (de)sodiation processes may also lead to uncertainties in terms of thermodn. or kinetic properties.  Herein, we present the first construction of SIBs based on inorg. fullerene-like (IF) MoS2 nanoparticles.  Closed-shell-type structures, represented by C60 fullerene, have largely been neglected for studies of alkali-metal hosting materials due to their inaccessibility for intercalating ions into the inner spaces.  However, IF-MoS2, with faceted surfaces, can diffuse sodium ions through the defective channels, thereby allowing reversible sodium ion intercalation/deintercalation.  Interestingly, Re-doped MoS2 showed good electrochem. performances with fast kinetics (ca. 74 mA h g-1 at 20 C).  N-type doping caused by Re substitution of Mo in IF-MoS2 revealed enhanced elec. cond. and an increased no. of diffusion defect sites. Despite recent great progress in finding appropriate electrolyte, an on-going research focus of the AIBs remains to be exploiting host electrodes for the large aluminum (complex) ions.  Herein, a star-shaped two-dimensional (2D) WS2 microsheet assembly cathode substitute is prepd. and applied in AIBs for the first time.                         cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Confinement growth of layered tungsten sulfide in hollow beaded carbon nanofibers with synergistic anchoring effect to reinforce lithium/ sodium storage performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Huayu; Chen, Xing; Qian, Chen; Yan, Hui; Yan, Chenyi; Xu, Nuo; Piao, Yuanzhe; Diao, Guowang; Chen, Ming</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>

                        cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Improved lithium and sodium ion storage properties of WS2 anode with three-layer shell structure</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Tingting; Guo, Ruisong; Luo, Yani; Li, Fuyun; Meng, Leichao; Sun, Xiaohong; Yang, Zhiwei; Luo, Honglin; Wan, Yizao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">331</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A three-layer shell structured W disulfide-based material for high capacity anode was prepd. by a hydrothermal method.  The three layers consist of a stable porous C shell (PCS), a W disulfide nanosheet (WS2) and a highly conductive N-doped graphene (NG).  While the nanostructured WS2 and NG ensured the fast Li/Na ions (Li+/Na+) insertion/extn., the stable PCS shell provides structural stability during cycling, resulted in the improved rate capability and long cycle life of the PCS/WS2/NG anode material.  For Li-ion batteries (LIBs), the capacity of PCS/WS2/NG anode is 324.7 mAh g-1 after 510 cycles at a high c.d. of 2 A g-1.  The discharge capacities of PCS/WS2/NG, PCS/WS2, WS2 and PCS anodes are 272.9, 105.1, 8.1 and 6.7 mAh g-1, resp., at a high c.d. of 4 A g-1.  When the c.d. rebound to 0.1 A g-1, their capacity retentions are 57.8%, 38.3%, 17.6% and 26.4%, resp.  The capacity retention of PCS/WS2/NG anode is 69.6%, higher than that of the pristine WS2 anode.  For Na-ion batteries (SIBs), PCS/WS2/NG anode exhibits a specific capacity of 205.0 mAh g-1 after the 900th cycle at the c.d. of 0.5 A g-1.  The excellent electrochem. performance of the three-layer shell structured PCS/WS2/NG nanomaterial is a promising anode for high performance LIBs and SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1016/j.jallcom.2018.07.008"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Niu, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">WS<sub>2</sub> Nanoflowers on Carbon Nanotube Vines with Enhanced Electrochemical Performances for Lithium and Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">WS2 nanoflowers on carbon nanotube vines with enhanced electrochemical performances for lithium and sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xin; Zhang, Jinying; Liu, Zechen; Fu, Chengcheng; Niu, Chunming</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Alloys and Compounds</span>

The as-synthesized precursors were further annealed at a low temp. in flowing argon to obtain the final materials (WS2/C composite).  The structure and morphol. of the WS2/C composite were characterized by x-ray diffraction, XPS, and SEM.  The electrochem. properties were tested by galvanostatic charge/discharge testing and a.c. (AC) impedance measurements. Furthermore, the amorphous WS2/C composite exhibited a lower charge/discharge plateau than bare WS2, which is more beneficial for use as an anode. This not only provides more active sites and open channels for the intercalation of Na+ but also facilitates the electronic and ionic diffusion.  Therefore, WS2 NWs exhibited an ultra-long cycle life with high capacity and rate capability in the potential window of 0.5-3 V.                         cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Cubic-shaped WS2 nanopetals on a Prussian blue derived nitrogen-doped carbon nanoporous framework for high performance sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lim, Yew Von; Wang, Ye; Kong, Dezhi; Guo, Lu; Wong, Jen It; Ang, L. K.; Yang, Hui Ying</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">21</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10406-10415</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Despite the cost-effectiveness of sodium sources, sodium-ion based electrochem. energy storage devices still have a few challenges in competing with lithium-ion based batteries (LIBs) for commercialization and practical applications.  In particular, the high rate performance and long cycling lifetimes are very difficult to be achieved in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Herein, we report a simple solvothermal method to prep. cubic-shaped nanostructures with vertical rose petal-like layers, which are used as anode materials in SIBs.  The well-designed WS2@NC structures consist of WS2 nanosheets and Prussian blue-derived nitrogen doped carbon nanocubic framework, which possess unique 2D WS2 nanosheets and are vertically grown on the well-defined 3D porous carbon hierarchical structures.  As anode materials for SIBs, this structure displayed high rate capacity at 384 and 151 mA h g-1 at 100 and 5000 mA g-1, resp.  More importantly, the performance of the electrode materials can be maintained at more than 200 cycles with coulombic efficiency not less than 99%.  The excellent electrochem. performance is attributed to the synergistic effect of the composites that enhances the electrochem. transport properties of the WS2 due to the well-defined, nano-structured hierarchical scaffolding and the highly conductive nature of the framework.  From the results shown, this unique design method provides unexplored insights into new and simple methods in improving the electrochem. performance of the 2D-TMDs based SIBs electrode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref47" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.1142/S1793292009001551"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zak, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sallacan-Ecker, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Margolin, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Genut, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tenne, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Insight into the Growth Mechanism of WS<sub>2</sub> Nanotubes in the Scaled-up Fluidized-Bed Reactor</span>. <i>Nano</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2009</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>04</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">91</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">98</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

(50-100 g/batch) of pure nanotubes, which appear as a fluffy powder, and (400-500 g/batch) of nanotubes/nanoplatelets mixt. (50:50), with nanotubes usually coming in bundles.  The two products are obtained simultaneously in the same reaction but are collected in different zones of the reactor, in a reproducible fashion.  The characterization of the nanotubes, which grow catalyst-free, by a no. of anal. techniques is reported.  The majority of the nanotubes range from 10 to 50 μm in length and 20-180 nm in diam.  The nanotubes reveal highly cryst. order, suggesting very good mech. behavior with numerous applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref48" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1016/j.jelechem.2021.115681"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Duan, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ahmed, W.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are a promising candidate for large-scale energy storage owing to the abundance and low cost of sodium resources. In this work, WS2/Ni3S2@N-doped carbon composite (WS2/Ni3S2@NC) was prepd. by using a simple solvothermal approach with subsequent annealing approach.                         cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Insight into the High-Temperature Cycling Stability of a Micro-nanostructured LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4/Graphene Composite Cathode for High-Voltage Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gao, Chao; Liu, Haiping; Bi, Sifu; Fan, Shanshan; Liu, Qian; Li, Huilin; Cao, Lixin; Luo, Chongxiao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
