        </div><div class="casAbstract">LiNi0.5Mn1.5O4 (LNMO) cathode has drawn enormous interest for its extraordinarily high voltage and low cost.  However, a high voltage leads to crit. side reactions significantly degrading the cycling stability and safety, which will be accelerated by a high temp.  Graphene coating has been demonstrated to be a valid method to tackle these problems mentioned above.  However, the enhancements mechanism on cycling stability remains unclear.  Thus, we meticulously constructed hybrid sphere-nanorod-like micro-nanostructured LNMO/graphene composites and deeply explored the improvement mechanism on the cycle performance of LNMO.  The results confirm that graphene coating contributes to protect the active substance from corrosion by electrolyte decompn. and helps stabilize the LNMO structure against structural deformation.  Owing to graphene coating, LNMO/graphene composites present a better electrochem. performance.  After circulation for 100 cycles at 55°C, LNMO/graphene composite (1000:5 wt) maintained 99.4% of its initial discharge capacity under 2 C.  Furthermore, such excellent electrochem. performance is also reflected in assembly LNMO/graphene/Li4Ti5O12 (LTO) full cells.  This work reveals the origins and enhancement mechanisms for LNMO/graphene composites, which can be extended to other graphene composites to promote the application of graphene.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref50" class="refNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit50" data-doi="10.1039/C7CP00934H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grinberg, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zussman, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Livneh, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zak, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Raman Scattering from Single WS<sub>2</sub> Nanotubes in Stretched PVDF Electrospun Fibers</span>. <i>Phys.                         cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Raman scattering from single WS2 nanotubes in stretched PVDF electrospun fibers</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Grinberg, Olga; Deng, Shengwei; Zussman, Eyal; Livneh, Tsachi; Zak, Alla</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Inorg. WS2 nanotubes (INT-WS2) were embedded into sub-μm polyvinylidene fluoride-co-hexafluropropylene (PVDF-HFP) electrospun fibers.  In this report we explore the Raman scattering spectroscopy from a single nanotube during stretching of individual nanocomposite fibers.  Red shifts of up to ∼4.7 cm-1 for A1g and E12g WS2 bands were found before reaching the "tearing point" of the fibers.  These shifts may correlate with up to ∼2.8% of the WS2 nanotube elongation.  Moreover, the absence of the A1g and E12g bands' broadening, as well as the nonappearance of the E22g shear mode in the nanotube Raman spectra, suggest the stretching of the nanotubes as a whole (including inner layers).  These results point to the excellent adhesion of the nanotubes' surface to the polymer and to the effective load transfer from the polymer to the WS2 nanotube.  In order to elucidate the nature of interaction between the polymer and the nanofiller, we modeled the deformation of composite fibers using an elastic lattice spring model (LSM).  The results of the model are fully consistent with our interpretation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref51" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.8b07596"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Di

                        cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Insight on the Enhanced Reversibility of a Multimetal Layered Oxide for Sodium-Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Di Lecce, Daniele; Campanella, Daniele; Hassoun, Jusef</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

Here, we show that the synergistic effects of various metals, enhanced structure, and optimal morphol. of Na0.48Al0.03Co0.18Ni0.18Mn0.47O2 material lead to remarkable reversibility in a Na cell.  X-ray diffraction refinement evidences that the electrode has a P3/P2-type layered structure, whereas SEM study shows a morphol. consisting of primary layers with nanometric thickness regularly stacked into uniform micrometric particles. Na+ intercalation into the layered oxide between 1.4 and 4.6 V vs. Na+/Na with relevant lattice stability.  The study shows the absence of phase transitions during Na+ exchange within the material framework, which advantageously leads to enhanced reversibility, benefiting from minor lattice change upon Na+ intercalation, fast diffusion, improved electrode/electrolyte interphase, and smooth voltage profile.  Hence, the electrode delivers a max. capacity of ∼175 mAh/g with suitable cycling stability and a Coulombic efficiency approaching 99% in a sodium cell.  Therefore, we believe that the study reported herein may shed light on important characteristics of this attractive class of electrodes, allowing efficient operation in next-generation Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref52" class="refNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit52" data-doi="10.1039/c2jm33992g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, S. Those characteristics make it a potential anode material for light and high-performance lithium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref59"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref59" class="refNumLink">57</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit59" data-doi="10.1002/chem.201501759"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">WS<sub>2</sub> Nanowires as a High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. - Eur. J.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>21</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">11878</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">11884</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/chem.201501759</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D21%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D11878-11884%26journal%3DChem.%2B-%2BEur.%2BJ.%26author%3DY.%2BLiu%26author%3DN.%2BZhang%26author%3DH.%2BKang%26author%3DM.%2BShang%26author%3DL.%2BJiao%26author%3DJ.%2BChen%26title%3DWS2%2BNanowires%2Bas%2Ba%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fchem.201501759&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c06295&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fchem.201501759&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit59R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">59</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">WS2 Nanowires as a High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yongchang; Zhang, Ning; Kang, Hongyan; Shang, Minghui; Jiao, Lifang; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry - A European Journal</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">21</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">33</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11878-11884</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CEUJED</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0947-6539</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the synthesis and anode application for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) of WS2 nanowires (WS2 NWs).  WS2 NWs with very thin diam. of ≈25 nm and expanded interlayer spacing of 0.83 nm were prepd. by using a facile solvothermal method followed by a heat treatment.  The as-prepd. WS2 NWs were evaluated as anode materials of SIBs in two potential windows of 0.01-2.5 V and 0.5-3 V.  WS2 NWs displayed a remarkable capacity (605.3 mA h/g at 100 mA/g) but with irreversible conversion reaction in the potential window of 0.01-2.5 V.  In comparison, WS2 NWs showed a reversible intercalation mechanism in the potential window of 0.5-3 V, in which the nanowire-framework is well maintained.  In the latter case, the interlayers of WS2 are gradually expanded and exfoliated during repeated charge-discharge cycling.  This not only provides more active sites and open channels for the intercalation of Na+ but also facilitates the electronic and ionic diffusion.  Therefore, WS2 NWs exhibited an ultra-long cycle life with high capacity and rate capability in the potential window of 0.5-3 V.  This study shows that WS2 NWs are promising as the anode materials of room-temp. SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref60" class="refNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit60" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2018.07.060"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gong, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Controllable Morphologies and Electrochemical Performances of Self-Assembled Nano-Honeycomb WS<sub>2</sub> Anodes Modified by Graphene Doping for Lithium and Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>142</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">697</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">706</span></span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D142%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D697-706%26journal%3DCarbon%26author%3DY.%2BSong%26author%3DJ.%2BLiao%26author%3DC.%2BChen%26author%3DJ.%2BYang%26author%3DJ.%2BChen%26author%3DF.%2BGong%26author%3DS.%2BWang%26author%3DZ.%2BXu%26author%3DM.%2BWu%26title%3DControllable%2BMorphologies%2Band%2BElectrochemical%2BPerformances%2Bof%2BSelf-Assembled%2BNano-Honeycomb%2BWS2%2BAnodes%2BModified%2Bby%2BGraphene%2BDoping%2Bfor%2BLithium%2Band%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c06295&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.carbon.2018.07.060&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Controllable morphologies and electrochemical performances of self-assembled nano-honeycomb WS2 anodes modified by graphene doping for lithium and sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Song, Yaochen; Liao, Jiaxuan; Chen, Cheng; Yang, Jian; Chen, Jinchen; Gong, Feng; Wang, Sizhe; Xu, Ziqiang; Wu, Mengqiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">142</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">697-706</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Self-assembled nano-honeycomb WS2 modified by graphene doping were prepd. by improved one step hydrothermal method.  In this hybrid structure, graphene plays a key role of transferring the morphol. from the nanowire microporous spheres to graphene-supported nano-honeycomb plane structure, which has the larger the sp. surface area and higher cond.  This anode delivers the superb electrochem. performances of lithium/sodium ion batteries with high specific charge capacity (953.1/522.3 mAh·g-1 at 0.1 A·g-1), long cycling life (more than 350/200 cycles at 1 A·g-1), and high charge/discharge rates (up to 10/5 A·g-1).  This nano-honeycomb structure WS2 composite anode with facile hydrothermal process, as well as superb electrochem. performances, makes it attractive for the potential applications in lithium/sodium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref57"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref57" class="refNumLink">59</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit57" data-doi="10.1007/s11671-010-9642-x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis and Characterization of Cobalt-Doped WS<sub>2</sub> Nanorods for Lithium Battery Applications</span>. <i>Nanoscale Res. Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2010</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">8</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">1301</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1306</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s11671-010-9642-x</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D5%26publication_year%3D2010%26pages%3D1301-1306%26journal%3DNanoscale%2BRes.%2BLett.%26issue%3D8%26author%3DS.%2BWang%26author%3DG.%2BLi%26author%3DG.%2BDu%26author%3DL.%2BLi%26author%3DX.%2BJiang%26author%3DC.%2BFeng%26author%3DZ.%2BGuo%26author%3DS.%2BKim%26title%3DSynthesis%2Band%2BCharacterization%2Bof%2BCobalt-Doped%2BWS2%2BNanorods%2Bfor%2BLithium%2BBattery%2BApplications%26doi%3D10.1007%252Fs11671-010-9642-x&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c06295&amp;doiOfLink=10.1007%2Fs11671-010-9642-x&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit57R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">57</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis and Characterization of Cobalt-Doped WS2 Nanorods for Lithium Battery Applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Shiquan; Li, Guohua; Du, Guodong; Li, Li; Jiang, Xueya; Feng, Chuanqi; Guo, Zaiping; Kim, Seungjoo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale Research Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1301-1306</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NRLAAD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1556-276X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cobalt-doped tungsten disulfide nanorods were synthesized by an approach involving exfoliation, intercalation, and the hydrothermal process, using com. WS2 powder as the precursor and n-butyllithium as the exfoliating reagent.  XRD results indicate that the crystal phase of the sample is 2H-WS2.  TEM images show that the sample consists of bamboo-like nanorods with a diam. of around 20 nm and a length of about 200 nm.  The Co-doped WS2 nanorods exhibit the reversible capacity of 568 mAh g-1 in a voltage range of 0.01-3.0 V vs. Li/Li+.  As an electrode material for the lithium battery, the Co-doped WS2 nanorods show enhanced charge capacity and cycling stability compared with the raw WS2 powder.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref58" class="refNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit58" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2021.138339"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ghosh, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qi, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Martha, S. K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pol, V. G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">WS<sub>2</sub> Anode in Na and K-Ion Battery: Effect of Upper Cut-off Potential on Electrochemical Performance</span>. <i>Electrochim. Acta</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>383</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">138339</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.electacta.2021.138339</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D383%26publication_year%3D2021%26pages%3D138339%26journal%3DElectrochim.%2BActa%26author%3DS.%2BGhosh%26author%3DZ.%2BQi%26author%3DH.%2BWang%26author%3DS.%2BK.%2BMartha%26author%3DV.%2BG.%2BPol%26title%3DWS2%2BAnode%2Bin%2BNa%2Band%2BK-Ion%2BBattery%253A%2BEffect%2Bof%2BUpper%2BCut-off%2BPotential%2Bon%2BElectrochemical%2BPerformance%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.electacta.2021.138339&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c06295&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.electacta.2021.138339&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit58R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">58</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">WS2 anode in Na and K-ion battery: Effect of upper cut-off potential on electrochemical performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ghosh, Sourav; Qi, Zhimin; Wang, Haiyan; Martha, Surendra K.; Pol, Vilas G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">383</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">138339</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs)-based layered anode materials are gaining attention because of their large interlayer spacing to host Na- and K-ions.  However, the capacity retention and coulombic efficiency are major obstacles for such rechargeable K-ion battery anodes.  Beside, understanding operating potential window employed during charge-discharge processes plays important role on the electrochem. performance.  The impact of operating potential window of WS2 electrodes during charge-discharge was studied in Na and K-ion battery.  WS2 nano-plates were synthesized using one step solid-state reaction under autogenic pressure at elevated temp. (RAPET) method.  The upper cut-off potential is restricted to 2.0, 2.5, and 3.0 V to understand the stability of the electrode.  The restriction of upper cut-off potential at 2.0 V, results into the capacity retention of 73% (180 mAh g-1) and 75% (250 mAh g-1) in K-ion and Na-ion batteries at the end of 50 cycles.  Further, kinetic anal. reveals that the K-ion storage in WS2 electrode is diffusion dominated due to intercalation of K-ion into host lattice, and Na-ion storage is pseudo-capacitive in nature.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript"         </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable hydrogen gas batteries show promises for the integration of renewable yet intermittent solar and wind electricity into the grid energy storage.  Here, we describe a rechargeable, high-rate, and long-life hydrogen gas battery that exploits a nanostructured lithium manganese oxide cathode and a hydrogen gas anode in an aq. electrolyte.  The proposed lithium manganese oxide-hydrogen battery shows a discharge potential of ~ 1.3 V, a remarkable rate of 50 C with Coulombic efficiency of ~ 99.8%, and a robust cycle life.  A systematic electrochem. study demonstrates the significance of the electrocatalytic hydrogen gas anode and reveals the charge storage mechanism of the lithium manganese oxide-hydrogen battery.  This work provides opportunities for the development of new rechargeable hydrogen batteries for the future grid-scale energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1002/adma.202110631"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Crystalline-Amorphous Interfaces Coupling of CoSe<sub>2</sub>/CoP with Optimized D-Band Center and Boosted Electrocatalytic Hydrogen Evolution</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Amorphous and heterojunction materials have been widely used in the field of electrocatalytic hydrogen evolution due to their unique physicochem. properties.  However, the current used individual strategy still has limited effects.  Hence efficient tailoring tactics with synergistic effect are highly desired.  Herein, the authors have realized the deep optimization of catalytic activity by a constructing cryst.-amorphous CoSe2/CoP heterojunction.  Benefiting from the strong electronic coupling at the interfaces, the d-band center of the material moves further down compared to its cryst.-cryst. counterpart, optimizing the valence state and the H adsorption of Co and lowering the kinetic barrier of hydrogen evolution reaction (HER).  The heterojunction shows an overpotential of 65 mV to drive a c.d. of 10 mA cm-2 in the acidic medium.  Besides, it also shows competitive properties in both neutral and basic media.  This work provides inspiration for optimizing the catalytic activity through combining a cryst. and amorphous heterojunction, which can be implemented for other transition metal compd. electrocatalysts.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202200855"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Realizing Negatively Charged Metal Atoms through Controllable D-Electron Transfer in Ternary Ir<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>Rh<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb Intermetallic Alloy for Hydrogen Evolution Reaction</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Alloying noble metals with non-noble metals is a promising method to fabricate catalysts, with the advantages of reduced noble metal usage and excellent activity.  In this work, electron-abundant Ir/Rh sites, as highly active centers for the hydrogen evolution reaction (HER), are realized by fabricating Ir1-xRhxSb alloys through the arc-melting method.  The electron transfer from Sb to Ir/Rh makes the latter neg. charged, leading to considerably optimized adsorption for active H species during HER.  As a result, the Ir1-xRhxSb alloy exhibits outstanding activity for HER, with an optimized overpotential of 22 mV at 10 mA cm-2 and a Tafel slope of 47.6 mV dec-1.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201909530"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Cathode Choice for Commercialization of Sodium-Ion Batteries: Layered Transition Metal Oxides versus Prussian Blue Analogs</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With the unprecedentedly increasing demand for renewable and clean energy sources, the sodium-ion battery (SIB) is emerging as an alternative or complementary energy storage candidate to the present com. lithium-ion battery due to the abundance and low cost of sodium resources.  Layered transition metal oxides and Prussian blue analogs are reviewed in terms of their com. potential as cathode materials for SIBs.  The recent progress in research on their half cells and full cells for the ultimate application in SIBs are summarized.  In addn., their electrochem. performance, suitability for scaling up, cost, and environmental concerns are compared in detail with a brief outlook on future prospects. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>10</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1902872</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tailoring the pore size of a polypropylene separator with a polymer having intrinsic nanoporosity for suppressing the polysulfide shuttle in lithium-sulfur batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yu, Xingwen; Wu, Hao; Koo, Joseph H.; Manthiram, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-sulfur (Li-S) batteries are being considered as one of the most promising candidates for the development of next-generation energy storage technologies.  Although much progress has been made over the past decade, the development of Li-S batteries is still held back by a crucial polysulfide-shuttle problem.  To address this crit. issue, an approach to reduce the pore size of the separator is presented here, to prevent the penetration of sol. polysulfide species.  A polymer with intrinsic nanoporosity (PIN) is developed within the micrometer-scale pores of a polypropylene separator.  The framework of polypropylene acts as a skeleton to sustain reliable mech. properties with the thin membrane.  Upon the formation of PIN in the pores, the polypropylene separator maintains its thickness.  With the thin PIN-polypropylene membrane, the Li-S cells can be operated with a relatively high sulfur loading.  The PIN allows the transport of Li+ ions, but suppresses the penetration of the polysulfide species.  The Li-S batteries with the PIN-modified polypropylene separator exhibit enhanced cycling performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201902485"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bresser, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Transition Metal Oxide Anodes for Electrochemical Energy Storage in Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) with outstanding energy and power d. have been extensively investigated in recent years, rendering them the most suitable energy storage technol. for application in emerging markets such as elec. vehicles and stationary storage.  More recently, sodium, one of the most abundant elements on earth, exhibiting similar physicochem. properties as lithium, has been gaining increasing attention for the development of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) in order to address the concern about Li availability and cost-esp. with regard to stationary applications for which size and vol. of the battery are of less importance.  Compared with traditional intercalation reactions, conversion reaction-based transition metal oxides (TMOs) are prospective anode materials for rechargeable batteries thanks to their low cost and high gravimetric specific capacities.  In this review, the recent progress and remaining challenges of conversion reactions for LIBs and SIBs are discussed, covering an overview about the different synthesis methods, morphol. characteristics, as well as their electrochem. performance. Rechargeable sodium/potassium-ion batteries (SIBs/PIBs) with abundant reserves of Na/K and low cost have been a promising substitution to com. lithium-ion batteries.  As for pivotal anode materials, metal sulfides (MSx) exhibit an inspiring potential due to the multitudinous redox storage mechanisms for SIBs/PIBs applications.  Nevertheless, they still confront several bottlenecks, such as the low elec. cond., poor ionic diffusivity, sluggish interfacial/surface reaction kinetics, and severe vol. expansion, which distinctly restrain the battery performance.  Meanwhile, the systematic insights into the design strategies of MSx for SIBs/PIBs have been seldom elaborated.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The effects of modifications in the synthesis Li/Co/dopant concns. on the performance and cycle life of Li Co oxide were studied to learn how different modification methods work in relation to each other and to provide data for up-to-date com. interest.  The LiCoO2 materials were prepd. using the same precursors and synthesis process to ensure the comparability.  The electrochem. characterizations were performed in both half-cells and LiCoO2/graphite pouch cells.  The Mg-Ti doped LiCoO2 shows superior performance compared to stoichiometric and over-lithiated LiCoO2.  The Mg-Ti doped sample shows 89% capacity retention after 1000 cycles in 3.0-4.2 V and 80% capacity retention after 240 cycles in 3.0-4.4 V in LiCoO2/graphite pouch cell.  The better rate capability is attributed to Ti doping reducing the Co valence in LiCoO2, making it more metallic and conductive.  The longer cycle life of the doped LiCoO2, in turn, is attributed to a better structural stability caused mainly by Mg doping.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Anode-active materials with higher reversible capacity than that of conventional graphite have been studied and the electrochem. performance of some C/Si-based materials was analyzed.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  A rapidly developing market for portable electronic devices and hybrid elec. vehicles requires an urgent supply of mature energy-storage systems.  As a result, Li-ion batteries and electrochem. capacitors have lately attracted broad attention.  Nevertheless, it is known that both devices have their own drawbacks.  With the fast development of nanoscience and nanotechnol., various structures and materials probably overcome the deficiencies of both devices to improve their electrochem. performance further.  In this Review, electrochem. storage mechanisms based on C materials for both Li-ion batteries and electrochem. capacitors are introduced.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Ultrahigh-Ni layered oxides hold great promise as high-energy-d. cathodes at an affordable cost for lithium-ion batteries, yet their practical application is greatly hampered by the poor cyclability.  Herein, by employing LiNi0.94Co0.06O2 as a model cathode in a full-cell configuration, the interphasial and structural evolution processes of ultrahigh-Ni layered oxides are systematically investigated over the course of their service life (1500 cycles).  By applying advanced analytic techniques (e.g., Li-isotope labeling, region-of-interest method), the dynamic chem. evolution on the cathode surface is revealed with spatial resoln., and the correlation between lattice distortion and cathode surface reactivity is established. Furthermore, the crit. impact of cathode surface reaction on the graphite anode-electrolyte interphase (AEI) is revealed at nanometer scale, and a universal chem./phys. evolution process of the AEI is illustrated, for the first time.  Finally, the practical viability of ultrahigh-Ni layered oxides is demonstrated through Al-doping strategy.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With increasing cost and supply risks of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), exploitation of alternative metal-ion batteries has been ongoing in recent years.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), potassium-ion batteries (PIBs), and aluminum-ion batteries (AIBs) have unique advantages as post-LIB alternatives.  Nonetheless, development of electrode materials with high capacity, good reversibility, and extended cycling stability remains challenging.  Metal sulfides (MSs), with improved electronic cond. and better reversibility than metal oxide counterparts, as well as high theor. capacities, are deemed as promising electrode materials and have been investigated in SIBs, PIBs, and AIBs. Recent advances using in situ characterization methods to probe the complex electrochem. mechanisms of MSs in SIBs, PIBs, and AIBs are also presented and highlighted. Herein, we report a simple solvothermal method to prep. cubic-shaped nanostructures with vertical rose petal-like layers, which are used as anode materials in SIBs. Graphene coating has been demonstrated to be a valid method to tackle these problems mentioned above.  However, the enhancements mechanism on cycling stability remains unclear.  Thus, we meticulously constructed hybrid sphere-nanorod-like micro-nanostructured LNMO/graphene composites and deeply explored the improvement mechanism on the cycle performance of LNMO. In this report we explore the Raman scattering spectroscopy from a single nanotube during stretching of individual nanocomposite fibers.  Red shifts of up to ∼4.7 cm-1 for A1g and E12g WS2 bands were found before reaching the "tearing point" of the fibers.  These shifts may correlate with up to ∼2.8% of the WS2 nanotube elongation.  Moreover, the absence of the A1g and E12g bands' broadening, as well as the nonappearance of the E22g shear mode in the nanotube Raman spectra, suggest the stretching of the nanotubes as a whole (including inner layers). In order to elucidate the nature of interaction between the polymer and the nanofiller, we modeled the deformation of composite fibers using an elastic lattice spring model (LSM). Beside, understanding operating potential window employed during charge-discharge processes plays important role on the electrochem. performance.  The impact of operating potential window of WS2 electrodes during charge-discharge was studied in Na and K-ion battery.  WS2 nano-plates were synthesized using one step solid-state reaction under autogenic pressure at elevated temp. (RAPET) method.  The upper cut-off potential is restricted to 2.0, 2.5, and 3.0 V to understand the stability of the electrode.  The restriction of upper cut-off potential at 2.0 V, results into the capacity retention of 73% (180 mAh g-1) and 75% (250 mAh g-1) in K-ion and Na-ion batteries at the end of 50 cycles.  Further, kinetic anal. reveals that the K-ion storage in WS2 electrode is diffusion dominated due to intercalation of K-ion into host lattice, and Na-ion storage is pseudo-capacitive in nature.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-3"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i12">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c06295?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c06295</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Tables summarizing the fitting results of the impedance spectra and summary of the electrochemical performance of published WS<sub>2</sub>-based anodes for LIBs and SIBs. TEM images and corresponding SAED patterns of NTs-WS2@C before and after cycling. (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c06295/suppl_file/am2c06295_si_001.pdf">am2c06295_si_001.pdf (424.94 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

        <script nonce="9318819c1a9254b8-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

        <script nonce="9318819c1a9254b8-SJC">
// LIT-455776 workaround
// Moving the popup before the header content for tab accessibility
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', () => {
    const headerNode = $('.header_content');
    const popupNode = $('.pairing-affiliations-popup');

    if (!headerNode.length || !popupNode.length)
        return;

    popupNode.insertBefore(headerNode);  
});

<script nonce="9318819c1a9254b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="9318819c1a9254b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function getCookie(name) {

      };

        <script nonce="9318819c1a9254b8-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});

$(document).ready(function(){
    var cookie = "";
    var uri = "/doi/full/10.1021/html_original_uri?id=123";
    console.log("ACSPubs2 start");
    $.getJSON('https://extreme-ip-lookup.com/json/', function(data, status) {
            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");
                });
                
            } else {
                    cookie=status;
            }

            cookie1 = cookie;
            cookie = cookie1 + "; path=/; domain=.acs.org";
            //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
        cookie="error: 127.0.0.1";
        //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
        $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
        console.log("ACSPubs2 failed");
        //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
    });
